Albom has fallen away from his faith, going so far as to marry outside of it. Reluctantly, Albom agrees to the eulogy and sets upon a series of interviews to get to know the rabbi on a personal level. More than theology lessons, the conversations seek to be a series of life lessons.<br /><br />As this journey begins, Albom seeks to do charity work in Detroit and crossed pathes with Henry Covington. Far from a model citizen in his early life, Covington finds himself the pastor of an inner-city church with a massive hole in the ceiling. Covington takes the most deperate into his church. The life and faith lessons he provides are equally profound. If you are sick, you are accepted in a hospital. If you are spiritually ill, a church should welcome you.<br /><br />Weaving the stories together, Albom creates an easy yet moving read about coming back to faith. On one hand, he seems to me reconciling the Christian and Jewish facets of his life. On a larger level, the evident message is that it is never too late to come back to faith.

B0016HNOXQ|4.0|If you were around in the 1990's, you heard Porishead's two studio albums, [[ASIN:B000001FI7 Dummy]] and [[ASIN:B000003TSP Portishead]] if you listened to new sounds and music. As grunge was fading, Portishead, along with Tricky and Massive Attack, put out electronic-based monliths that had meat on the bone. \\"Eletronica\\" was a marketing term, but Poritshead's deep, subliminal, and truly frightening soundscapes were unrivaled. Their ability to create creepiness by keeping the sound small but packing it with hidden layers make their first two outings classics. Their music was not music with electronic production placed for trend value, but electronic darkness from the ground up, that created emotion from the music out.<br /><br />In 2008, the band came back for Third. Just when the blackness cannot get anymore black, blacker it gets.<br />The band follows the same blueprint to a large extent: minimal basic tracks with wads of sound packed into the music's most hidden corners. Here, the instrumentation is more organic, and effective: the gauzy tremilo guitars, Beth Gibbon's piercing flat voice, its detachment from the music making  the tracks that much more emotional. Listen to \\"The Rip,\\" where the jump-cut, muted percussion creates the fragmentation of hallucinations, your most psychotic nightmares. These are the dreams you tell NO ONE!!<br /><br />The electronic sheen is strongly present, but less glossy, and the playing is starker, more blunt in its horror. You really have to get all of Portisheads work, and live with it a very long time, let it seep under your skin.It may sound the same at first, but this band has the mark of any great band--the differences will emerge as the subtleties imbed, and the fold opens. Slowly. Bleakly.

1439107955|5.0|Eradicate, a good word when it comes to cancer.Unfortunately, I don't think it can be or will be applied to this pox on living organisms for some time to come.My views are based on actual practice experience and now my views are deftly backed up by this wonderful book.From the first time I read a review on it and followed its progress I knew it would do well and it did, it won the Pulizer as it should.The author, an oncologist himself did write something unique here.A look at the history of and development of the cancer treatments and difficulties we know right up to today.He did his research and knows his stuff and in between rounds and after work was able to put together an expansive look and explanation of just what we are dealing with here.Yes, there are external factors which cause or contribute but mostly it is a game of chance with your genes.One wrong switch flips on because of a mutant gene and the cascade begins. Well, it is really not that simple but then again in a complete reductionist view, it can be.Life it seems is really a crap shoot when it comes to cancer, smoke, drink, eat fatty foods and enjoy your bar-b-que because you just might live to 100. Run, exersize, eat the right foods,live well,don't smoke and you could be dead by 40 from untreatable cancer of whatever.Some things can be handled nicely by medicine and some despite surgery,chemo,prayer, whatever, too bad your dead. The book is very inclusive and has something for everyone as it can be quite technical at times and rather pedestrian at others.One thing I will say is that it was well written  for someone with no experience writing before.It will appeal to all walks of life, from the medical clinician to the nurse on the floor to the common person curious about a very taboo topic even today.For those who want to know more about this thing we call cancer and just why with all the money thrown at it we have yet to make truly significant inroads by way of a cure.It does drag somewhat in the middle but stay with it because it will leave you with a better overall knowledge of the subject and will hopefully enlighten you and give those who do suffer hope.This is a must read for medical or nursing students interested in Oncology. For the general public, an explanation and examination of what was and what is, far,far, better than anything our Surgeon General could deliver.Truly, one of the better books yet written on this thorny subject recently and probably will be for some time to come.As was said above,cancer is and always was with us, perhaps it is natures way of thinning the herd from within our bodies rather than from without.We can define it, slow it,put it into remission status,even some can be eliminated almost to the point where we believe that is it gone forever but the lingering fear of return is always present isn't it?. What we can't do unfortunately is say we can eradicate it from the human condition once and forever.Early in my career I worked on an oncology floor and found it to be utterly depressing, full of pain, suffering,heartache and difficult in many ways.You work and work but the result was always the same, death.Sometimes quick,sometimes lingering and slow.True, there are some successes but once cancer has you in its grip, well,the outcome is not always good. Those who work in this field have my utmost respect  and admiration as it is one of the most difficult jobs I've ever experienced. This book will show you why that is so in a broad and comprehensive manner.

B0044XV3QY|3.0|First off, let me say, I really loved this movie.  It was a brilliant achievement for the technical and artistic wizardry that brought an alien world to life on screen in such lavish detail, and with such spectacular visual beauty.  Moreover, the storytelling was great as well.  The movie was well paced, the characters' motivations believable (mostly), the acting was top notch, and the characters that the actors portrayed were (for the non-villains at least) well-drawn, likeable characters with whom the viewer can identify.  All of this makes for a stunning cinematic experience, and I immensely enjoyed watching the film.  For its technical wizardry, and in the sense of  it's been an interesting story, well told, the film deserves five stars.<br /><br />Having said that, however, there is something about the movie that irritated me intensely, and I'm not seeing it mentioned in many of the mostly gushing reviews here, so I guess I will have to be the voice in the wilderness (no pun intended).  In \\"Avatar\\" we are treated to yet another round of the old \\"noble savage\\" myth.  The term's been around since at least the seventeenth century (though the actual idea is much, much older - read Tacitus' comments on how Germanic barbarians had certain virtues that the jaded, decadent Romans of his day lacked, for example), and one would think we'd have learned better than to indulge in such condescending romantic nonsense by now.  Basically, the idea is that people are essentially good and virtuous by nature, and advanced civilization and its institutions are what causes them to lose their way.  This is certainly the interpretation this movie supports, where the Na'vi are seen as unqualifiedly noble, virtuous, unselfish, people, unsullied by greed, rapacity, cruelty, etc. and living in perfect harmony with nature, understanding to the Nth degree the part they are supposed to play in their world's ecosystem.  By contrast, the earth-people - civilized people - are capable of being good, like Sully and Grace and Trudy, but are just as often callous, arrogant, greedy, self-righteous, and so on, and who, in the end, behave like Nazi Einsatzkommandos.<br /><br />The first problem with this is that the idealized noble savage, of the sort depicted here, never existed.  Archaeology reveals evidence of violence and warfare in the earliest and most primitive societies.  There never was a primeval Eden where people lived peacefully in primitive, yet enlightened commune with nature, perfectly in harmony with their world.  Archeology, again, shows that primitive man could be, and usually was, just as destructive of his environment as modern man, it simply took him longer to use up his resources with only primitive technology at his disposal.  And what living in primitive societies actually means is average lifespans of 29, women giving birth to a dozen children in the hopes that at least half of them will live out their first year; it means being helpless when smallpox or cholera or typhus or bubonic plague swoops down on your community and carries off half the population.  It also means superstition and ignorance, and so, perhaps things like, say, a nice human sacrifice to take away the anger of the gods and persuade them to give you a better harvest this year, since the last one was so poor that many of the community starved to death over the winter.  Primitive life is no idyllic dream.  It NEVER was.<br /><br />The second problem with this movie is that it is a clumsy commentary on the history of colonialism, in the form of a fantasy tailored specifically for white people living with a heavy load of liberal, white guilt. And what is ironic is that, entirely unintentionally I'm sure, the film ultimately marginalizes indigenous peoples and affirms white supremacy - this ought to come as no surprise, I suppose, since a great deal of the modern liberal/progressive self-righteous moral preening turns out to be incredibly patronizing when you look at it inthe harsh light of reality.  And what do we see in this movie, when we look at it in that critical light?  Jake Sully is white redemption incarnate - liberal guilt enfleshed.  He serves to redeem the human race in general, and white Europeans in particular, by proving that at least some of us (guilty liberals, naturally) are good.  This, I'm sure, is Cameron's intentional message.  But his unintentional one is that white liberals like him aren't merely good, they're SUPERIOR to just about everybody else.  Look what happens as the movie unfolds: Sully is turns out not only to be a superior human being, he is also a superior Na'vi.  He does what none of these poor natives can do.  After enduring a brief period of contempt and ostracism for the part he played in the land grab perpetrated by the greedy humans, he redeems himself in spectacular fashion by performing a feat beyond the ability of any living Na'vi; he tames the most feared predator  in the skies of Pandora, thereby proving himself to be the most skillful, most courageous warrior imaginable, according to the Na'vi view of things.  He follows this up by assuming leadership of the Na'Vi, and working out their strategy.  Then, of course, his performance in the ensuing fight shows him to be their most valuable player on the battlefield too.  Then, with all the other Na'vi contenders for leadership conveniently dead, Sully not only assumes permanent leadership, he wins the heart of the most desirable girl in the tribe for good measure. The Na'vi now worship the ground he walks on.  As one commentary I read put it: \\"This is a classic scenario you've seen in non-scifi epics from Dances With Wolves to The Last Samurai, where a white guy manages to get himself accepted into a closed society of people of color and eventually becomes its most awesome member.\\"  The natives still end up with a white guy as their leader; it just happens in a kinder, gentler way.  And, of course, being a guilty liberal, he proves to be an enlightened leader, rather than an oppressor.  The natives are now better off than ever.<br /><br />It's a story written, as I've said, by and for guilty white liberals.  If a story like this were written by the colonized people of color themselves, something tells me there would be no white redeemer; the hero would come from within their own ranks, and when the battle was over and won, they'd continue with their lives, under their own leadership, with no humans/white Europeans in sight, whether of the guilty liberal variety or not.  Film makers like Cameron think they are being so just, noble, fair, and enlightened, by rubbing our noses in our own (admittedly) dark past.  But when you look at the story they tell when trying to do it, what you see them do, over and over, and again, probably entirely without meaning to, is rediscovering a sneaky way of turning every story about people of color into a story about ever so noble white people (like them, of course) -- and it's a story that doesn't entirely rewrite the history of colonialism either; they still get to be top dog, at the end.  But what else can you expect?  Self-righteous people, unsurprisingly, usually turn out to be self-centered as well.the harsh light of reality.  And what do we see in this movie, when we look at it in that critical light?  Jake Sully is white redemption incarnate - liberal guilt enfleshed. B004EBUXSA|5.0|Very fast, and those effective 8 cores allow you to do many things at once, all at full speed. There also is a 2700K model available for $50 more. That'll give a 4% faster CPU for $50. Whoopie. You'll never see it unless you run a benchmark.<br /><br />Pro:<br />There are 2 issues controlling the speed you get from a modern, multi-core CPU: The number of cores and the speed of each core. This CPU runs 4 cores, each of which your operating system can hyper-thread, giving 8 effective cores. The multiple cores allow several demanding processes each to run at full core speed, and so the speed of each core determines how fast each of those applications runs. This chip is a winner on both counts. But you'll need Windows 7 x64 or 64-bit Linux to take maximum advantage of what this chip offers. It really screams.<br /><br />For we techno-geeks, following are some benchmarks: Floating point calculations are critical to the performance of my image processing and numerical analysis work, so I ran my standard double-precision floating point benchmarks on a single core running at the stock speed under Windows 7x64 Pro and 1600MHz DDR3. This benchmark mixes different proportions of add/subtract/multiply/divide operations doing real-world calculations. A mix of [50%,0%,50%,0%] -- that is, only adds and multiplies, which run very fast -- gives 4.8 Gigaflops (billion floating point operations per second) per core. For a [43,29,14,14] mix, which is relatively heavy on the slow operation of division, I get 1.6 Gigaflops per core, which still is exceptional. Putting this in perspective, the stock i7-2600k gives 10x the performance of a Cray YMP supercomputer of 1992, and is more than 1000 times faster than my Sun Sparcstation of the same era. On the other hand, the stock i7-2600k is around 1/4000 as fast as the Fujitsu \\"K computer\\" in Japan, the world's fastest supercomputer that eats 12 Megawatts of power and costs $10 million/year to run.<br /><br />Con:<br />The CPU fan that Intel provided was a bit noisy. I could hear it wind up in RPM when the CPU came under a heavy computing load. For a few bucks I replaced it and all was good. Mine was probably a fluke, so don't let that get in the way of purchasing an i7-2600k.<br /><br />In summary: Get this chip --- it's a dream. You'll not find a significantly faster CPU near this price for at least a couple of years.

1400064627|5.0|This story begins in the 1980s with two women who meet in the waiting room  at the Rose Terrace Nursing Home in Birmingham, Alabama. Mrs.Ninny(Virginia) Threadgoode is a wise and charming lady who resides in the nursing home.  Evelyn Couch is a middle-aged woman, who comes weekly with her husband to visit her mother-in-law, Big Momma. She doesn't have very much patience with her mother-in-law and so she leaves and goes to the waiting room.  It is there that she meets Mrs. Threadgoode.<br /><br />Mrs. Threadgoode begins by going back in time and tells Evelyn about her life in the 1930s in Whistle Stop, Alabama, where they have one cafe named The Whistle Stop Cafe, one convenience store and one Bulletin called The Weems Weekly written by Dot Weems.<br /><br />Evelyn, at first, is not interested in listening to Mrs. Threadgoode's life history, as she has her own problems.  She is in her mid-forties, overweight and generally unhappy with her life.  She is very naive and not able to adapt to the changes in life. One would say that she is stuck in time and old-fashioned.  She is also bored and can't seem to fit in.  Because she is so miserable, she uses food as a way of coping.  She fills up on chocolate bars, ice cream and of course, gains more weight.<br /><br />Every week, Mrs. Threadgoode continues with her story back in Whistle Stop and Evelyn is beginning to show interest.  She tells her about The Whistle Stop Cafe, where all the folks meet aand talk about the goings-on in the town.  The Cafe is owned and run by two women, Idgie and her best friend, Ruth.  The cooking is done by two black women and Big George makes the barbecue.  This is rare with racism going on in the South.  Some of the Southern foods served are simply \\"to die for\\" like fried catfish, fried green tomatoes, dumplings, black-eyed peas, cobbler and grits, just to name a few.  There are recipes in the back of the book, which gives the book a nice touch.  It also adds to the book's southern flavor and gives it a homey feeling.<br /><br />Idgie is a tomboy, who likes to dress masculine and play practical jokes. One day at the Baptist Church, she puts poker chips in the collection box.  Both she and Ruth are their in thirties.  They are very close friends and some may say they have an intimate relationship.  The door at The Whistle Stop Cafe is opened to everyone from black people, hobos and the poor.  Only one thing, black folks have to enter through the kitchen door in the back or the Cafe would be burned to the ground by the KKK.<br /><br />There are excerpts from The Weems Weekly Bulletin over each chapter that has to do with Whistle Stop.  The paper gives the town folks all the latest news.<br /><br />With Evelyn's weekly visits and listening to Mrs. Threadgoode continue with her uplifting stories about her life, Evelyn's spirits improve.  She starts to make changes in her life, beginning with losing weight.  She is now filled with hope for the future.<br /><br />This novel has a lot to do with race relations in the South, homosexuality,<br />domestic abuse, an unsolved murder and aging. On the other hand, it is filled with strong friendships, fun times and  acceptance of people.<br /><br />Fannie Flagg is a Southern born gifted storyteller, whose books will lift your spirits with her quirky and lovable characters.  You will laugh a little, cry a little, but her book will keep you entertained from beginning to end.  This book is a WINNER.<br /><br />P,S.  If you enjoyed this book, you will also like Can't Wait To Get To Heaven.gives it a homey feeling.<br /><br />Idgie is a tomboy, who likes to dress masculine and play practical jokes. B005LAIGZU|4.0|There may be spoilers.<br /><br />From what I've read co-directors Mike McCoy and Scott Waugh were given uncommon access to military knowhow and technology, specifically the U. S. Navy SEALs.  Their original goal was to produce a quasi-documentary/recruiting film.  That evolved into a full length commercial film.  From that perspective I suspect they hit their mark.  There are some unusual aspects to this movie.  With some obvious exceptions, the primary cast is made up of real life active SEALs.  It is pretty clear they are not actors but I'm inclined to give them plenty of slack in that area.  I doubt real actors could have delivered some of the combat lingo and battle maneuvers like the real deal either.<br /><br />SEAL Team 7 is assigned a mission to rescue a kidnapped CIA agent (Roselyn Sanchez, TV's \\"Without a Trace\\") from terrorists located in Costa Rica.  She had been close to uncovering a jihadist plot to infiltrate the U. S. from Mexico.  In a bit of a sticking point, prior to their departure the filmmakers spend an unusual amount of time showing the men with their families so that we know they are just regular guys.  They have wives and kids.  You know just guys no different than any other neighbor.  It was a little much.<br /><br />What isn't overdone are the action sequences.  They are absolutely thrilling.  I was especially taken with the initial mission to rescue Agent Morales (Sanchez).  This involved parachuting in at night, navigating through the jungle and swamp and taking out the capturers with machine-like precision.  The first-person-video-game-like helmet-cam brought a snicker at first but it isn't overdone.  In a heart-pounding escape, the team is overwhelmed with bad guys, but is also aided by swift boats dropped into the river by huge helicopters.  So cool.  The bad guys have some big guns, but so does the Navy and they know how to use them.  Subsequently, a retrieved Blackberry leads the commanders to uncover details about the bigger plot.<br /><br />Working with a Russian smuggler, a Chechnya terrorist has lined up 16 Philippine suicide bombers to wear vests filled with explosive ceramic ball bearings and ignite them in various U. S. cities.  As the mission continues, the SEAL Team takes out half the terrorists in one location but must move near the U. S. border in Mexicali to prevent the remainder from tunneling in under the protection of the local drug cartel.  Working with their Mexican counterparts this final battle will have many casualties including some good guys.  I must admit I loved the action sequences in this movie, but the screenplay is a clunker.  Frankly it is irrelevant.  This is the best military recruiting film since \\"Top Gun\\" and even with its flaws is worth seeing.<br /><br />BLU RAY UPDATE (6-10-14)<br /><br />This second viewing on Blu ray provides reinforcement on some of my initial comments.  The film purposely is one-sided but I knew that from the beginning.  And I have to say, the best part are the action sequences, especially the first rescue of the captured CIA officer.  As the bad guys are about to outgun the escaping SEALs, they run into 2 swift boats armed with .50 cal. machine guns.  Game over.  Regardless of one's political beliefs, this is an adrenaline inducing scene.  On to the Blu ray disc itself.<br /><br />The video has a 1080p resolution with a 2.40:1 aspect ratio.  Using small digital cameras, the filmmaker's you-are-there feel comes across beautifully in this transfer.  Colors and skin tones are perfect.  Jungle greens and desert tans come across accurately.  In some of the night sequences, you might find a bit of visual noise but it's not distracting.  With a non-stop action flick like this you would hope to get a solid audio track.  And boy do you.  It comes in DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 and it's a stunner.  The viewer is caught in the middle of the constant action sequences and the sound onslaught is everywhere.  Surprisingly, at least to me, the film has anexcellent musical score thanks to Nathan Furst and it plays well when the scenes call for it.  Top shelf, demo quality audio.  Subtitles come in Spanish and English SDH. 1439153663|5.0|Kathleen Grissom opens the novel with a horrific prologue and then goes back ten years to recount the events leading up to the prologue.<br /><br />The story begins in 1791 and is told by the two main characters, Lavinia and Belle in alternating chapters.<br /><br />Two Irish immigrants die on their journey from Ireland to America on the ship that is owned by Captain James Pyke.  He is also the Captain of the ship.  They leave behind two children, a son, Cardigan and a daughter, Lavinia.  Because their parents didn't pay their passage, the children are separated.  Cardigan is easy to place and is sold and sent to another city to work as a slave, while Lavinia, who is almost seven years old , sick, frail and scared to death is taken by the Captain and is handed over to Belle on the Tall Oaks tobacco plantation in Southern Virginia, which he owns.<br /><br />Belle is the illegitimate half-white daughter of the Captain and she runs the Kitchen House, which is considered to be a place of privilege on the plantation.<br /><br />For seven years of her life, Belle is raised by her grandmother,the Captain's mother, in the BIG HOUSE. Her white grandmother accepts Belle as her granddaughter and teaches her everything, just like a white girl.  She learns how to read, write and how to behave.  Their relationship is one of love for each other.  Then one day her grandmother dies suddenly.  Belle is shocked and takes the death badly.<br /><br />Captain Pyke has never married and one day decides to bring home a young wife named Martha, who is twenty years to his forty years.  Belle is then evicted from his home.  She moves to the Kitchen House, because he doesn't want his young wife to know.  The Captain is absent most of the time and his young wife has to oversee the plantation.  He and his wife, Miss Martha have two children, Marshall and Sally.  Marshall grows up to be an alcoholic and violent man, who causes turmoil wherever he goes.<br /><br />Some of the people living in the Kitchen House are Mama Mae, the matriarch, Papa George, the patriarch, their children, Uncle Jacob and Ben.  Mama Mae is in charge of the women slaves.  She teaches Belle how to cook and now, Belle presides over the Kitchen House and cooks for the Big House.  Belle loves Mama Mae and Papa George. She would rather stay at the place she calls home than accept the \\"Freedom Papers\\". 0545265355|4.0|[...]      I just finished reading all three Hunger Games books. I know that I'm a little behind the times, but with some many books on my plate reading them hasn't happened until now. I love dystopian books. If you enjoy a nice everything gets completely better happily ever after type book, then this isn't for you. Like all good anti-utopia books, the Hunger Games shows us extremes of today's societal faults.The world that Suzanne Collins creates is terrifying. One can't help but thinking if some of the dangers of Panem are already being seen here in America.  The United States and other well to do countries are very much like the capitol with the rest of the world barely with enough to live on. The world powers are nations of excess in pursuit of trivial entertainment. No we do not pit our children against one another in a Mad Max Beyond Thunder Dome type arena in which 12 kids enter one kid leaves. The games are just a way of showing even greater social problems. The books show that even within the Capital there are the haves and have-nots.      I personally did not care too much for the main heroine, Katniss. Although I do think her development is more consistent with a real person put under extremely stressful circumstances. She just doesn't come across as particularly nice or likeable; a fault even the character will admit to. She has occasional acts of deeming quality. She does have a strength of character that stands in contrast to her environment. Also a fierce protective instinct of those whom she deems unable to defend themselves.Overall her lack of ability to acknowledge emotions other than fear and mistrust make her a little annoying to read since the story is written in first person. Collins actually made a character come across as both whiny and abrasive. Maybe her internal monologue was the problem; had the story been written in third person Katniss may not have been such an unlikeable character. I can't imaging trying to develop a love triangle witha main love interest like her. Peeta and Gale, the boys vying for young Katniss's affection, are both very likable. Peeta has this natural upbeat personality that brings a ray of sunshine to otherwise downtrodden cast of character's. His major fault being his complete devotion to Katniss. Even when he finds out that the love between them that sparks in the arena is all for show, he is hurt but still loves her. He is the embodiment of the phrase love is blind. It takes being brainwashed for him to question his love for Katniss, something even he overcomes in the end. Their games mentor, Haymitch, has it right when he tells Katniss that she doesn't deserve him. Gale on the other hand is a little more realistic in his devotion to Katniss. He gets angry with her and challenges her. My major complaint with The Hunger Games is that the characters don't rise above their circumstances very much. They remain strong enough to bring about a revolution and bring about change, but their evolution ends in beaten down, broken people; not people healing and leading their world after over throwing the government.      The story development was rather good. I actually read all three books in three days time. The plot was very captivating with plenty of action and drama. Some critics thought the story line was a little too Running Man for them, but I disagree. The games are a tool to keep its citizens completely at the mercy of its government, not a form of punishment for criminals and rebels. Panem makes its citizen watch the games and propaganda to instill fear. If this is what they do in retaliation for one uprising, what lengths would they go to if a second one failed. This is in addition to the other accidents, paltry living conditions, and segregation between districts. Panem keeps it's citizens too hungry and without a means to rally to help keep control. In the first book, I just wanted Katniss and Peeta to make it back to their loved ones alive.      As the second book developed, I kept hoping Katniss would want to fight back against the Capital. She wants to run away and keep everyone safe. Gale luckily doesn't want just safety for his family, but all of District 12. Since Katniss is seen as a unifying force due to her small rebellion at the end of book one, the president wants to make an example out of her and other victors by making them fight to the death again. She loses her chance to escape and resigns herself to not making it home again. Her only goal becomes repaying a debt she feels she owes to Peeta by trying to keep him alive. Peeta is so devoted to Katniss that you hope that she will start to love him also. I believe she does, but just doesn't allow herself the luxury of feeling for him. She writes her true feelings off as part of her debt to him. It is very frustrating to read. Catching Fire ends with a bang, with a small introduction into Rebel forces freeing some of the victors.      The introduction of the Rebels leaves the reader needing to find out how it all ends. Katniss needs to make choices as to what her part will be in the resistance and which young man she wants to be with. In the end she finds herself backed into a corner and takes a role with the resistance as her only recourse. I think the story might have developed better if Katniss had made a concrete choice. Mockingjay shows all the bad sides of war. The tragic loss of innocent life on both sides. The extremes in which even the so called good guys will go to in which to become victorious. How when you replace one government with another, you never really know if the new one will be better.      All in all, I recommend all three books with a Mad Hatter rating. The Hunger Games was my personal favorite of the trio.

The director (who also did Gattaca) provides us with a number of the adages and questions that apply to our own system, such as,  \\"Is it theft to steal what is already stolen?\\" and  \\"Not everyone can be immortal (rich).\\" This latter statement is shot down early in the movie when a disillusioned character exposes the \\"truth,\\" saying, \\"there is enough for everyone.\\"  In our current era of technology, in which wealth and poverty is not a problem of scarcity, but distribution, I found this willingness to examine the issue in this way to be refreshing.<br /> Perhaps most believable of all was the way that society had accommodated this impossible culture. We humans are creatures of habit.  \\"THE POWER OF HABIT: WHY WE DO WHAT WE DO AND HOW TO CHANGE IT\\" describes how successful corporations manipulate their customers through analyzing and exploiting habit.  Habits are described in terms of cue, routine, reward--and belief.  Belief is a major contributor to Alcoholics Anonymous' success (not 100%, but far more effective than merely treating chemical dependence) and the other major key is the power of group habits.<br />According to author Charles Duhigg, habits are not only individual--but social: family habits, clubs, neighborhoods, ethnic groups, states, nations, corporations--each human group brings different habits.  Rosa Parks's refusal to give up her seat on that Montgomery bus sparked a city-wide boycott because Rosa Parks was connected to diverse groups in the city--and these groups got together and formed an effective protest.  \\"The Power of Habits\\" explains why earlier protests in support of Blacks having the same civil rights as other citizens failed, and why the boycott sparked by Rosa Parks' arrest succeeded.<br />Starbucks couldn't FORCE people to spend $10 for a cup of coffee--but with a dedicated sales staff that puts Customer Service first, last and always people WANT to plunk down their hard-earned money and then leave a tip, too.  Duhigg tells how a kid from a broken home, someone who didn't have any chance of success, became a Starbucks manager through the Starbucks training program.<br />How did a toothpaste company contribute to National Defense?  How did concentrating on employee safety make ALCOA more productive and profitable?  How did a US Army commander in Iraq end the riots in his town without gunfire?  Read \\"The Power of Habits\\" and find out.<br />The edition provided through the Amazon Vine program was an Advance Reader's Copy and lacked an index and the table of contents lacked page numbers. She lives in a fenced-in coal mining district, one of 12 that exist solely to serve the Capital.  Life in her district is one of struggle for basic sustenance and survival.  What emerges from these conditions is a girl of strength and fierce independence.  She is certainly a flawed character, yet her awareness (and overestimation) of her own flaws is part of what makes Katniss so compelling.<br /><br />Katniss has had to use violence to survive, namely through bow hunting.  When she steps into the titular Hunger Games and has to fight other humans to the death, this acclimation to violence becomes her saving grace, even while it battles with her innate compassion.  There is romance here, but the romance does not consume her, this is not a story of a girl who loses herself to love.<br /><br />Anyone who has young daughters, I highly recommend giving this book to your children as soon as they are old enough to handle the violence.  Katniss, living as she does in a distopian world, can't be a role model exactly, but she can provide young girls with the knowledge that they are far more than just who they date and how pretty they are.  This book shows the value of inner strength tempered with self-reflection and compassion.<br /><br />We may live in a world far removed from District 12, but it is one where violence still exists and valor is still occasionally needed.  I would keep this on the shelf right next to the Handmaid's Tale to remind us to appreciate the freedoms we enjoy and remain ever wary of what we could become. 1451627289|5.0|11/22/63  by Stephen King<br /><br />Is Stephen King God? He creates new worlds that seem like our own, at first, then populates them with creatures -- human and inhuman -- bestowed with powers and problems that complicate their lives in otherworldly ways that seem perfectly believable.<br /><br />Like God, he conjures characters we grow to love and root for, and then cruelly kills them off, but in his latest novel, 11/22/63, he exercises his god-like powers to bring back to life the ones he chooses to resurrect.<br /><br />Reading a Stephen King novel is like being blind-folded and pushed through a strange doorway into a room from a different universe, but in 11/22/63, the doorway is a portal that drops the hero, Jake Epping, a high school English teacher, back in time to September, 1958, over and over again.<br /><br />Wouldn't you like to be able to travel back into the past to a simpler time, knowing what you know now (like next year's Kentucky Derby winner), and try to fix things that went wrong? Jake Epping's quest takes him to Dallas in 1963, in an attempt to save JFK from assassination by Lee Harvey Oswald. But knowing Stephen King as we do, no good deed goes unpunished, and there are always consequences.<br /><br />If he hadn't titled this book, 11/22/63, the date of the original assassination of JFK, he might have named it, Life Turns On A Dime, referring to a recurring theme in the book, how seemingly inconsequential acts can have historic effects on people's lives. It might also have been titled, The Philosophy of Stephen King, since, as he ages and matures; his life philosophy continues to gel, infusing his works with his beliefs. Love conquers all.<br /><br />Stephen King is a romantic. Romance and true love fuel his characters, juxtaposed next to horrid examples of wife-beaters and murderers who epitomize hate. His characterization of assassin Lee Harvey Oswald's love for his young daughter on one hand, and his brutality toward his beautiful Russian wife, Marina, on the other, makes the reader want to cheer when he finally gets what he has coming.<br /><br />After creating fifty novels or so, one wonders how he can keep doing it, but Stephen King has done it again. I couldn't put it down.<br />Charlie Norman

0307719219|3.0|My worst subject in college, economics struck me as either childishly obvious or as impenetrably arcane.  For example, the classic definition of price - where the supply curve meets the demand curve - in the abstract seems tautological, but how in heaven's name does one derive either curve?  Also, the units seem to change.  Dollars per what?  Number of widgets per what?  Furthermore, this definition has limitations when one considers that current rising supplies of oil to steady or falling demand does not lead to falling prices.<br /><br />You can state the book's thesis easily.  A sufficiently centralized government of inclusive, pluralistic political institutions reinforces inclusive economic institutions (a so-called \\"virtuous circle\\").  Exclusionary, extractive (ie, exploitive) political institutions reinforce extractive economic institutions (a \\"vicious circle\\").  A chaotic, decentralized polity tends to foster local extractive elites and economic markets and cannot establish the rule of law, especially equality before the law, the protection of property rights, and so on, necessary for general economic incentive or inclusive economic institutions.  In cases where extractive political institutions try to foster inclusive markets, as in the Peoples' Republic of China, we can show that such initial prosperity cannot be sustained.  The rest of the book consists of historical illustrations.<br /><br />You would think that a link between politics and economics would be mind-numbingly obvious, but apparently not among economists.  Acemoglu and Robinson spend the first part of the book demolishing various competing theories:  climate determinism, education levels, modernization, among others.  I did like very much the authors' emphasis that propitious conditions are historically contingent, rather than determined.<br /><br />The book has received a chorus of praise from both conservative and liberal economists.  The conservatives like the authors' emphasis on democratic institutions and free markets as essential for general prosperity, as well as the approval of both the British and American economies that runs through the book.  I'm not sure exactly what the liberals like, unless it's the role of government in markets.<br /><br />However, why is it that you can make a case for social scientists as a class - along with New Agers and politicians - as in the running for the Worst Writers in the World?  The sentences are at best utilitarian, at worst downright ugly.  In its current form, this is one bloated, repetitious book, at least 100 pages too long.  The organization is such that the book reads like a set of bagpipes slowly blatting out air.  The authors make no attempt to build an argument over the long haul.  Instead, they make their argument at the beginning and then keep remaking it.  It reminds me of the awful old teacher's dictum:  \\"Tell them.  Tell them again.  Summarize what you told them.\\"  The structure should have been more dynamic, building from point to point over the long haul, so that the reader gets a sense of discovery, a sense of payoff at the end.<br /><br />Even more interesting is what the authors *don't* say.  In general, I agree with their points about the reinforcing properties of the virtuous and vicious circles.  Inclusive and extractive institutions tend to stay that way.  However, they cite examples where vicious circles have been broken - Brazil, for example - and if true for vicious circles, why not for virtuous ones?  Consider, for example, that US voting rights are being restricted in various states under the false banner of voter fraud and that it takes millions to run for federal office, most of which is now supplied by a handful of very, very rich people.  Consider that the American middle class continues to be gutted as more and more (even the educated, or at least degreed) fall into poverty levels.  Consider that these situations have been prolonged and indeed encouraged by legislative unwillingness in both major parties to annoy the funding sources that perpetuate a political class, increasingly above many of the laws the general population is subject to.  I have no idea what the answer to any of this is.  I do believe that I cannot trust the political establishment, Republican or Democratic, to work toward a solution, since they have obfuscated rather than meaningfully legislated.  We may have to follow the Brazilian model:  set up local inclusive institutions, network with other places, and gradually build a political movement from the ground up.  The Tea Party and Occupy I think were broken attempts at this.  The Tea Party quickly became co-opted.  Occupy never recognized the need for stronger organization.<br /><br />At any rate, I believe the US is rapidly moving toward exclusionary politics and extractive economics.  We can hide, but we can't run - either away or for office.

The book aims to answer the question, why are some nations rich and some nations poor. How does it do?  It depends on what you're looking for.<br /><br />To start off, were given a couple of theories that the authors disagree with. For example, the theory that geography is the reason for nations prosperity or poverty. They then provide a short argument against those theories.  This part is not a large portion of the book.<br /><br />As I got into the meat of the book, I kept thinking it was an introduction to their thesis. I read about halfway through the book before I realized, they weren't just offering some evidence before making a bold pronouncement and then proving it. Instead, almost the entire book is evidence with very few specific claims made. For the most part, they just talk about extractive and inclusive economic elements without specifically getting into what those elements might be. If you're looking for something that sets up the target, aims you at it, and drives it home by the numbers, this isn't the book for you.  If you're a big fan of history and want to read a whole lot of information to be guided gently as to the author's conclusions, then you'll quite enjoy their approach.<br /><br />At no point reading this book was I bored. Yes there's a lot of \\"repetition\\" with similar examples of the same point and it meanders a bit to reach its arguments, but I was happily willing to go along for the ride.  I loved every single page and every bit of information they had about history, economics, and various cultures.  It was worth reading if only for the difference between the colonization of North and South America. I'd never considered some of the differences or why things turned out the way they did. The authors make a very compelling argument about the extracted nature of the governments the Spanish installed versus the (not due to altruism, but necessity) more inclusive ones started in North America and potentially explains why in some very oil-rich countries the general population is still so poor.  The historical and economic analysis of Africa was equally intriguing.<br /><br />So to reiterate, if you're looking for something that presents a concise theory both quickly and directly that's not what you'll get here.  If you're looking for something that will give you some really interesting aspects of history you may not already be familiar with and you don't mind the scenic route to the point, then you'll probably enjoy this book as much as I did.Spanish installed versus the (not due to altruism, but necessity) more inclusive ones started in North America and potentially explains why in some very oil-rich countries the general population is still so poor. MASTER SPOILER: The answers in order are sort of, still don't know, and hopefully you didn't notice that CGI head because you're going to see a lot of it.<br /><br />ENCOM, the game company from the first film, is an Apple-like proxy in the second; its board has lost its way, arrogantly above all reproach and releasing the same operating system with few improvements (it's up to OS-12).  They refuse to release the OS for free, a reminder of the old LINUX debate.  Early in the movie it seems like Tron: Legacy might have a lot to say about the state of technology.<br /><br />But then Sam Flynn (Garrett Hedlund) gets sucked into the program and everyone is poorly lit. The light cycles are back, the giant flying staplers are back, the Frisbees are back, but Master Control is oddly nowhere to be found.  We have a new villain in CLU, who features the same Jeff Bridges digitized head that we saw in the flashback.  Tron: Legacy features flashbacks both to reality and within the mainframe, which makes for a series of jarring transitions.  I half-expected the flashbacks to look like pixilated Atari 2600-style graphics.<br /><br />What we get is Bridges as a digital version of the Dude from The Big Lebowski and wide-eyed hottie Quorra (Olivia Wilde) lounging in various cyber-chic poses. The plot -- oh right, this is a film with a plot! - is that our real-life Users must make it back to the portal to the outside world before CLU uses it to transport his digital army back through it.<br /><br />Tron's entire premise pivots on your ability to buy into this universe.  There's a new race of programs called the ISOs who \\"spontaneously evolved\\" and were subsequently killed off.  CLU is processing other rogue programs to be Stormtrooper-like followers, but it's never made clear what defines a \\"rogue\\" program.  Programs hang out at bars, eat food, paw at each other (porn?) and there are even homeless drunk programs (Internet?).<br /><br />CLU's plan isn't really explained...will his followers have any weapons when they show up in the real world?  It looks like he plans to invade Earth with a few thousand troops.  Dangerous, sure, but enough to take over the world?  Not even close.  The original Tron movie confined its ambitions to technology we could understand: blackmail, video games, and broadcasts of secret code.  Tron: Legacy blows all that in the first five minutes and instead turns into space invaders.<br /><br />As a laser light effects show, Tron: Legacy is amazing, but two hours is simply too much.  As a coherent movie, it's largely the Marix with more glowy parts: There's the dangerous chick in leather who guards our clueless hero, the villain who wants to create rigid conformity, the battle to escape one world to enter the next, and a lot of spiritual mumbo-jumbo about the nature of the soul and its role in the universe.<br /><br />Bridges spouts most of that pseudo-philosophy here, and it's hard not to giggle when he says, \\"you're messing with my Zen thing, man.\\" At just over two hours this superficial film will mess with your Zen thing too.port his digital army back through it.<br /><br />Tron's entire premise pivots on your ability to buy into this universe. B004LUNZ6Q|5.0|I compare these two recordings for several main reasons: they usually head the field in recommendations for the best version to buy; they were the first two I heard, almost simultaneously, when as an teenager I was first becoming enraptured by opera; lastly, I happen to own and play both and listen to them with pretty much equal enjoyment. Neither is perfect but they certainly do justice to this wonderful period-piece which made the sadly short-lived Weber famous throughout Europe.<br /><br />I first heard the Wolf's Glen scene in an EMI sampler LP of operatic highlights and still feel that it is the most atmospheric ever recorded - better than the Kleiber version which, although still effective, doesn't quite capture the Gothic chill and has a rather ordinary-voiced Samiel compared with the hard, abrasive Fritz Hoppe for Keilberth. Where Kleiber particularly scores is in the crowd scenes with the dances and choruses. It was a on a remainder bin LP - only the first LP of the set - that I heard his overture and the opening scene with its natural-sounding crowd noises, mocking laughter and galumphing peasants struck me as enormously invigorating music-making; Kleiber takes the whole thing at a clip and really injects life into proceedings. Keilberth is steadier and more staid, with the grander-sounding BPO but his is also an affectionate and beautifully played account.<br /><br />I give Kleiber five stars even though I readily admit an antipathy to Peter Schreier's throaty, nasal tenor. I really cannot comprehend how some find his pinched voice beautiful but he is impassioned and characterful here, sounding really desperate as Max, so I let it pass. Schock has a more conventionally baritonal heroic tenor and sings agreeably if a tad blandly. Dramatic verisimilitude is enhanced by the fact that Keilberth's singers deliver their own dialogue whereas for Kleiber a very truncated script is employed and actors are used, with the usual mismatch between singing and speaking voices. That abridgement also means that some famous lines are cut, such as that quoted by Bismarck in the Reichstag in 1849: \\"Glaubst du, dieser Adler sei dir geschenkt?\\" (\\"Do you think you get this eagle for nothing?\\").<br /><br />Regarding the leading ladies in both versions, all are superb. There is little to choose between two of the loveliest German post-war sopranos in Elisabeth Gr&uuml;mmer and Gundula Janowitz, both of whom were lyric sopranos who could sing the lighter Wagner roles and to whose silvery voices I could listen all night. Both Lisa Otto and Edith Mathis are delightful as &Auml;nnchen. I prefer Karl Christian Kohn's mostly focused, black-voiced Kaspar for Keilberth to Theo Adam (sometimes unkindly known by his non-admirers as \\"Mr Wobble\\", but passable for Kleiber). Both hermits are impressive, even if a younger-voiced Franz Crass is steadier than the veteran Gottlob Frick - but both bring the requisite kindly gravitas to the role. The Leipzig chorus is livelier and interpretatively freer than the more \\"operatic\\" Berliners; the latter are less animated in their hailing of Kilian and barracking of Max but only comparatively speaking and they respond credibly in little incidents such as when Max draws a knife on Kilian. Both conductors relish the Bauer element in the music. There are a few clumsy tape edits on the EMI recording and its 1958 stereo sound, although fine, is less immediate than the fuller but slightly toppy DG recording from 1973. (I do not know if the remastering of the latest EMI issue has corrected those edits - can they, in fact?)<br /><br />DG persists in gracing the Originals cover with what is a basic solecism: a double-barrelled shotgun rather than a marksman's rifle. I cannot choose between these two admirable versions but can endorse both as excellent, faithful renditions which do honour to a seminal German opera.voices. 0385474547|4.0|The themes in \\"Things Fall Apart\\" were powerful, especially the deterioration of a traditional society once outsiders brought their own values into the culture. Well-meaning foreigners brought their own religions and Western customs into a traditional African culture they viewed as backwards or savage, and in need of redemption and modernization. I never understood the deeper meaning of colonialism before I read this book. I was also left with a deeper understanding of indigenous cultures and a realization that people who lived in traditional societies didn't have anything broken that needed to be fixed by the colonialists.<br /><br />The characters aren't particularly likable, but I don't believe the author intended to write a book with admirable or lovable characters. Truly, some parts of the book were excruciatingly dull, but the importance of \\"Things Fall Apart\\" is the history behind it, even though the book is a work of fiction. If the book is required reading for a literature course, I highly recommend purchasing a study guide to accompany the text. This is a book that needs a roadmap if you're going to spend a serious amount of time analyzing it for a research or term paper.

B00003OSU4|5.0|~ Bert Stern only made one movie in his career, and it is visually stunning.  From the start it is clear that Bert Stern is a visual artist with a great understanding of perspective, angle, selective focus, and image composition.  That is what made him such a successful photographer.  He set out to produce a document of a special weekend in Newport, and he succeeded.  He did a great job of incorporating scenes from the America's Cup trials of that weekend along with scenes of the local people going about their wealthy lives plus the unique event of the jazz festival.  He was able to subtly show the co-existence of the ultra-rich at leisure and the musicians at work.  Through images of the crowd he was able to show the fact that at an event such as a jazz festival people of all races and ages could enjoy each others company - in the 1950's in America that was not always the case.<br />~ The bonus features include an extensive interview with Bert Stern that is enlightening.  Not only does he outline his concepts in putting together the documentary but he gives credit where credit is due when he talks about the contribution of the Avakian brothers.  That interview also clearly indicates his intent to make this something other than just a concert film.  In addition to the interview on the DVD, the liner notes accompanying the DVD provide futher insight.<br />~ Others have written about the fact that this is not a \\"concert film\\" in the broadest sense of that phrase, and Stern makes it clear that he did not intend it to be strictly a \\"concert film\\".  For that reason, people looking for a film that documents all or most of the performances from that weekend are rightfully disappointed.  However, the film does give us some wonderful moments and the opportunity to watch performances by artists who we would not otherwise be able to see.  Where else can you see video of Jimmy Giuffre with Bob Brookmeyer or Chico Hamilton's band practicing or Chuck Berry improvising with a clarinetist or the absolutely stunning sight of Mahalia Jackson casting a calming spell over an audience by singing \\"The Lord's Prayer\\". By editing together the images of Miss Jackson and her captivated crowd, Stern is able to demonstrate the power of music.  Of course, this is the movie in which Anita O'Day delivers her marvelous performance that rocketed her to international stardom and brought her to the attention of a broad audience.<br />~ The insert liner notes and the interview also indicate that there are tens of thousands of feet of film archived somewhere waiting to be explored.  Hopefully someday someone will take up the challenge and go through all that film and give us a more complete view of the music from that weekend.  But for now we should be thankful that we have this beautifully captured and excellently edited document of a time when people went to a concert unencumbered by electronic devices, e-mail, text messages, etc and all they did was enjoy the music.inetist or the absolutely stunning sight of Mahalia Jackson casting a calming spell over an audience by singing \\"The Lord's Prayer\\". B006OFN0BQ|3.0|Let's face it: J. Edgar Hoover is one of the more controversial figures of the 20th Century.  He was the top lawman in the nation, but he often resorted to unsavory tactics to bring down the bad guys (and kept files on a bunch of the \\"good\\" guys whose views he distrusted, such as JFK and Martin Luther King, Jr.).  He was a complex human being: hero and villain at the same time, insecure but powerful, distrusting even of his own emotions.  No one film could ever capture such a figure.<br /><br />J. EDGAR tries.  It really does.  Dustin Lance Black's (MILK) script is solid, if not quite eventful.  Despite such a volatile central figure, the closest thing to a climactic moment comes between Hoover and his rumored lover, Clyde Tolson; that would be great, if this were a film about Hoover's possible homosexuality, but it isn't.  Nor is it a film about Hoover's political subterfuge.  Or the founding of the Federal Bureau of Investigation.  It's a film about all of that, which means it ends up, really, being a film about none of it.  It tries encompassing too much, and in the end, it's as though everyone involved unanimously agreed that Hoover was far too complex for that.  So they settled for something a little watered-down.<br /><br />That's not to say this film doesn't have its merits.  It's another solid piece of filmmaking from director Clint Eastwood. Likewise, Leonardo DiCaprio gives a layered performance at the titular character; it's not his best, either, but it's worth watching.  The best moments of the film come from the inferiority Hoover feels from interacting with others, namely Judi Dench as his mother, Armie Hammer as Tolson, and Naomi Watts as trustworthy secretary Helen Gandy.  It is in these moments that J. EDGAR shows glimmers of what it could have been.  Unfortunately, each relationship could easily have been its own film, and that's not even brushing the various political angles.  As is, J. EDGAR is a film that tries to do too much--but at least you know you have a solid, dependable crew behind at the helm.een its own film, and that's not even brushing the various political angles. 1416583432|4.0|Psychologist and self-proclaimed dog lover, Alexandra Horowitz turned the research world inside out when she suggested to her PhD adviser a study of the ordinary dog. In academia the baboon was a worthier research subject. Determined Horowitz pursued an eight year study of man`s best friend. Although a plethora of breed and training books are marketed yearly, there is little serious research on our canine companion. This book fills in some of the gaps. The author challenges the reader to view the world from the canine's point of view or umwelt.  The result is an enjoyable and informative read for a general audience.<br />  The book blends anecdotal and scientific material as it meanders to closure. Editing some of the research material on other animals may pick up the pace. Objective points in each chapter are illustrated by linkage to memories of her beloved dog, Pump. Horowitz validates her well documented research by making it personally relevant.<br />  Some of the following points are particularly interesting. Although Horowitz acknowledges a DNA link, less than one percent, between dog and wolf in the distant past(145,000 years ago), she does not subscribe to current view of dogs as pack animals. She devotes a significant part of the book exploring physical, developmental and social differences between the dog and its progenitor the wolf. Her conclusion is no canids are as fully domesticated as the dog.<br />  This year's Westminster show recognized 2,500 breeds.  According to the author, classifications of AKC breeds are genetically inaccurate. Cairin terriers share much of their genomes with hounds, shepherds and mastiffs.<br />  Contrary to popular belief, dogs are not color blind although their vision is somewhat muted. They see objects at a distance better than close-up, but sight is not a dog's primary sense. Humans have six million olfactory receptors. Depending on the breed, dogs can have over three hundred million. Two other misconceptions are clarified. Dogs do not urinate to mark territory but to pass on important information. Posture rather than size determines dominance.<br />  Alexandra Horowitz has written a solid book that demands readers to view the world through the dog's senses. I don't wish a pregnancy on any teenager, but I'd wish that kind of support for any who do end up as mommy-to-be, the kind of support that too few girls in trouble seem to get. That happy background keeps makes the comedy possible, and the edgy humor makes this movie succeed.<br /><br />-- wiredweird
B005B19UPE|4.0|I believe Holocaust novels are a double-edged sword in mainstream literature. Certainly they are ripe for good drama and would appeal to masses that way; however, they are certainly not feel-good reads, which may put a damper on sales. With Sarah's Key, however, Tatiana de Rosnay has crafted a beautifully bittersweet story that is part mystery, part tragedy and overall an absorbing and suspenseful read.<br /><br />Julia Jarmond is a journalist living in Paris, readying herself to move into an old apartment that her husband has inherited. When she is assigned to write an article about the Vel' d'Hiv Roundup--a Nazi-decreed raid and mass arrest that took place in Paris in July 1942--she discovers that the apartment was inhabited by her in-laws, the Tezacs, after the previous residents, a Jewish family, were arrested. Her investigation leads her to uncover secrets buried by the Tezac family for generations, and she develops an insatiable fascination with the Jewish family's daughter, Sarah Starzynski, who was just ten at the time of the Roundup and disappeared without a trace, never confirmed dead or alive in concentration camp records.<br /><br />The novel tells Sarah's and Julia's stories simultaneously, cross-cutting between time periods; from the Vel' d'Hiv Roundup to Julia's personal and journalistic endeavors. This method effectively generates suspense for Sarah's storyline, though Julia's takes a while to pick up the pace and become as absorbing as the former. While Julia's story begins with the inspection of her new apartment, Sarah's begins with being woken up on that fateful night in 1942 by the French police; the inciting incident that draws readers in immediately.<br /><br />At times the novel suffers from occasional plot contrivance and drama-provoking gimmicks, which are needless, as the story is fairly strong enough on its own. There are also times when predictability dampens whatever power the plot twists and sudden turns of events carry. When a writer has engaging characters and a methodically conceived plot, there is no need for dramatic stunts and affectations to keep readers turning the page.<br /><br />Sarah's Key is a flawed yet worthwhile read; moving, riveting, and successfully maintaining, for the most part, a balancing act between the suspense of a detective novel and the tragic elements that are to be expected of a Holocaust novel. Readers will want discover along with Julia what became of Sarah Starzynski; whether she became a camp statistic or the heroine of her own inspirational story of survival.<br /><br />You can read this and other reviews at my blog: [...]and a methodically conceived plot, there is no need for dramatic stunts and affectations to keep readers turning the page. 0061537969|5.0|Chances are this novel's target audience has not read a book from a dog's point of view since the children's classic, The Poky Little Puppy, or Eric Hill's Spot the Dog series. Nonetheless, The Art of Racing in the Rain is beautifully written, moving, funny and surprisingly philosophical, and its appeal is not limited to dog-lovers. Garth Stein has crafted an exquisite story with an endlessly charming narrator, and provides a deeply affecting look into the mind of a dog with a human soul.<br /><br />Enzo, an elder dog awaiting the end of his days, lives in Seattle with his beloved owner, Denny Swift, a mechanic and racecar driver, and relates stories of the Swift family's trials and tribulations over the years. From his adoption as a puppy to Denny's marriage, the birth of his daughter, his wife's illness, the tragedy and hardships that follow, and all the racetrack happenings in between, Enzo narrates with the wisdom of a wordless observer, with humanlike clarity and perception, even while bound by basic canine needs. Believing he will someday be reincarnated as a man, Enzo makes it a priority to observe human behavior and gain knowledge to carry with him to his next life.<br /><br />Stein often uses the racetrack as a metaphorical representation of life itself, as Enzo shares Denny's passion for racecars and often compares the Swift family's dysfunction to the challenges faced while racing under trying circumstances. In addition to the mind of a dog, readers get an inside look into the mind and mentality of a racer and the balance they must achieve between stability and the need for speed.<br /><br />Enzo's personality adds a whimsical quality to the narration. He's part crotchety elder, part adventurous youth; both a sworn protector of his family and a dependent house pet; a dog who prides himself on being well-behaved and civilized, yet possesses animal instincts that sometimes drive him to disobedience. His frustration at being unable to speak to humans is palpable, though he assures himself that he compensates by being a good listener. Added for comic relief is his longing for opposable thumbs.<br /><br />Heartrending yet ultimately uplifting, The Art of Racing in the Rain captivates with its painfully accurate portrait of the joys, tragedies and absurdities of human life, and its meditation on what it means to be human, all shown through the eyes of a winning protagonist. That adds modern characters and plot to the story, but allows the movie to skip through his life, selecting the scenes that contribute to his curious story.<br /><br />The basic theme is star-crossed lovers. More accurately, crossed lives, living their lives in opposite directions. They meet when each is just a few years old, but that friendship lasts their lifetime. As each approaches the other from the opposite end of the calendar, though, they have a tragically short time for a normal relationship. Although their relationship has long breaks, they continue it anyway, even when it can't be normal. Parts of this will resonate strongly with anyone who's kept love alive even when bodies and minds fail.<br /><br />That love pervades this movie. B0067LY4WG|4.0|While Cohen's songwriting or voice has never been an issue for me, his production since I'm Your Man has left a lot to be desired. Cheesy backing vocals, synths,drum machines and that damn Casio. As with his debut & Songs From a Room, Cohen as a performer works best in intimate, stark settings. Old Ideas is a welcome return to that. So on that front, cry \\"hallellujah\\". As for the album as a whole, not every song is great but more often than not, it's exemplary Cohen.<br /><br />Where his last, Dear Heather was disjointed & underwhelming, Old Ideas is more cohesive & substantial. Cohen's wry, if world weary sense of humor is often overlooked by critics & fans alike. Going Home greets you with, \\"I love to speak to Leonard/ he's a sportsman & a shepherd/ he's a lazy bastard/ living in a suit\\". While the closing time gypsy tango of Amen might clock in over 7 minutes, it's a new Cohen classic. Not bad for a 78 year old. Show Me the Place quietly left me stopping in my tracks. The ominous, driving Darkness is another classic in my book, and it's a relief to hear an acoustic guitar figure so prominently on a Leonard Cohen album again. The other major standout is Crazy to Love you.<br /><br />Any \\"Greatest Hits\\" mix would be incomplete without any of the aformentioned. Banjo manages to be both catchy & haunting while the gospel tinged Come Healing is a bit cloying owing to the backing vocals. Lullaby is just that despite the fact that \\"the wind in the trees is talking in tongues\\".<br /><br />The fact that Different Sides doesn't put a button on the ending, may leave some listeners unsatisfied. Seems the intent was to leave things unresolved, as opposed to neatly tied in a bow. Depending on your mood, it pays off--- or not.<br /><br />While quiet & laid back, there's  a dark undertow to these songs. As with all of Cohen's best work, he's not going to shout it from the mountaintops so much as whisper it in you ear just before closing time. As a longtime fan, Old Ideas has left me a very satisfied one indeed. For me, his best since 1979's Recent Songs.t me a very satisfied one indeed. For me, his best since 1979's Recent Songs.

1401201911|4.0|When I heard the premise--baby Superman landing in the Soviet Union--I imagined something very different from what this graphic novel ultimately delivered.<br /><br />Owing to the mid-century influence of the Comics Code Authority, Superman was the champion of a United States in which the American ideal was perfectly realized. I imagined that Red Son would give a similar treatment to the USSR, with young Kal-El growing into the champion of the society of mutual-aid. We could witness Batman and Superman transform from state-sponsored enemies to men with begrudging respect for each other's ideals and abilities. Backed by the good-will of the American people, Lex Luthor wouldn't be forced to sink into depravity, and could just possibly illustrate the moral virtue of the American way of life.<br /><br />Given the prospect of conquering all capitalist opposition, Superman might be forced to struggle with how his presence is preventing the predicted transition of the Soviet Union from socialism (a classless but state-run society) into communism (a classless and state-free society). He might be forced to come to terms with the fact that, as a Nietzschen \\"overman\\", he has no place in the Marxist world he is helping to create. That thread is explored a bit, but I was hoping for some authentic and ruthlessly boring political and moral philosophizing in the tradition of so many Russian authors.<br /><br />Instead, Millar keeps the man of steel much closer to canon. I understand that Red Son was not published under the Vertigo label, and that it fits into a tradition of alternate-universe comic book stories. Given that, I can appreciate a story about a Soviet Superman written with American sensibilities. I can appreciate why Smallville, Jimmy Olsen, and Lois Lane appear in this story, and the particular philosophizing Millar does explore.<br /><br />There you are. If you were hoping for the former but will accept the latter, it's a decent read.

0679746323|3.0|\\"Mao...was responsible for well over 70 million deaths in peace time.\\"<br /><br />Then Chang and Halliday spent 650 pages detailing the count. A chilling portrait emerged from pages and pages of bodies: this one man, driven by his lust for power and domination, relentlessly left people to die, watched people to die, tricked people to die, and sent people to die. Lives were nothing to him but a number, one that he betted casually in a game of thrones with style. \\"China is ready anytime to sacrifice half of its population for the worthy cause,\\" he announced to the applause of millions to be sacrificed, time and again. In a different time, I could be one of them -- such statements were thought of conveying a sense of honor and courage, which above all, would inspire a sense of pride. And they did. Chang and Halliday, however, told us that perhaps, they were meant literally.<br /><br />For the chilling sensation alone, it was a superb book. I know I will never look at the history of China since 1920 in the same way.<br /><br />But that's about it. The book is more artistic than historic, more fictional than truthful, and more colorful than deep. Chang personally hates Mao, who was responsible for the ruin of her family, and it shows in every line of the book. Emotional authors write powerful books, but emotional historians...nay, there is no such thing as an emotional historian. Passion does not sit through hundreds and thousands of old files for both sides of the story. Chang and Halliday claim they \\"researched\\" for 10 years for this book: interviewing numerous people, unearthing old papers, collecting data from all sources. The book indeed is full of fresh material that I have not seen anywhere else. The \\"Kaihui papers\\", for example, were quoted in their candid entirety. Still, this is no research. Many materials cannot be trusted. Historians would be obliged to verify their sources, or at least cite them. Such practice is not respected in this book. The incredulous phone transcription of Ye Qun, for example, was simply explained as \\"a three-hour telephone conversation that was bugged\\". Much more were quoted out of context, selected without justification, or \\"inferred\\" without bases. Take the death toll of the great leap forward. It is extrapolated from the consensus, a whopping 37.67 million. It could very well be a close estimate, but Chang and Halliday do not even try to convince the readers it is so. This is a topic that has been the subject of lots of serious scholarly research, yet the book does not mention any of them, nor offer any useful insight as to how their number compares to others. Even in history one should not get away without a minimal literature review. And oh, stuff like the \\"communist sleepers\\"...I don't know what to say. It is beyond being incredulous. I cannot imagine what could possibly rationalize such behavior, except that it fits in the conspiracy story the authors seem to enjoy?<br /><br />Chang and Halliday have a hypothesis, and they only see evidence consistent with that hypothesis, which, in turn, strengthens their beliefs in the hypothesis even more.<br /><br />Anyway, so what if this hypothesis is true? Well, it might as well be. What's disappointing is that the book starts and stops at it. Saying, or even proving Mao is a sociopath is not particularly deep. How did such a monster rise to the power? How could he manage to maintain his ruling sitting on so many bodies? Besides people dying, what else happened during the five decades?<br /><br />But Chang and Halliday were not interested in these questions. It's a tremendously focused book. \\"Today, Mao's portrait and his corpse still dominate Tiananmen Square in the heart of the Chinese capital. The current Communist regime declares itself to be Mao's heir and fiercely perpetuates the myth of Mao.\\" Closing the book, I look at the portrait of Mao on the cover, and feel the weighty, silent declaration of war of one couple against a nation's legacy.It is brave.

1451648537|5.0|Very well written biography on a fascinating life.  Steve Jobs is a compelling character.  On the one hand I found myself in constant awe of his genius (and anyone who tries to say he wasn't a genius is kidding themselves).  It would be one thing if he got lucky by collecting the right group of people and developing one hit product.  But this was a man who consistently delivered his vision to the consumers with many hit products spanning different industries.  You would be hard pressed to find anyone who did what he did.  That's not lucky.  On the other hand I found myself wondering why he had to be such a jerk.  I understand that in running a business of any size leaders have to make tough decisions that often times negatively impact the individuals working for them.  However, it became obvious early on that Steve Jobs would purposely treat people badly at times when he didn't need to and he would take it to a personal level.  What's interesting is that both parts, the genius with the vision and the jerk, stemmed from his dominant personality trait..his desire to be in control.  The size of the book may look big, but Walter Isaacson does a great job taking you on the journey.  I highly recommend this book.
